Rather than focusing on rockets and ships, like SpaceX and Blue Origin, Astrolab is producing rovers that can operate on interplanetary bodies, describing itself as the "UPS of the Moon."\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/07/17/moon-space-stoke-astrolab-spacex-blue-origin-musk/\n\nSource 3:\nThe idea, according to Matthews, is that as space tech develops, a cottage industry of logistics will be necessary to help support the economics of new development built on outer space surfaces. "It\'s going to open up access to places like the Moon at scale to enable industrial activity," he said. \n\nWhile Astrolab may not be a direct competitor to Blue Origin and SpaceX, another startup—Stoke Space—is working on similar technology with the aim of building fully reusable rockets. \n\nSpeaking at Brainstorm Tech, Stoke\'s chief operating officer Kelly Hennig admitted that she might not beat SpaceX to the goal, but that space onlookers should "vote for the little man, too." She said that as the space landscape evolves, there will be a need for both large freight companies as well as smaller firms, comparing Stoke to the Sprinter vans used by Amazon <https://fortune.com/company/amazon-com/>. Stoke raised <https://www.stokespace.com/stoke-space-series-b/> a $100 million Series B funding round in late 2023.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/07/17/moon-space-stoke-astrolab-spacex-blue-origin-musk/\n\nSource 4:\nIn late August, officials at NASA announced what had already become apparent: Two U.S. astronauts, stranded for nearly three months at the International Space Station, weren\'t coming home as planned and would have to remain in space for several months.\n\nThe Boeing <https://fortune.com/company/boeing/> Starliner craft that transported Suni Williams and Barry Wilmore to the ISS <https://fortune.com/company/iss/> in June, the company’s first crewed mission, had experienced multiple problems and was returning to Earth without people on board. A SpaceX <https://fortune.com/company/spacex/> rescue craft, meanwhile, couldn’t reach the astronauts until February 2025.\n\nThe only option for Williams and Wilmore was to wait. But that may not always be the case. And if and when the scenario changes, it may well be in a direction that is quite literally the stuff of science fiction.\n\nThe technology is there\n\nFor more than a century in theory and at least a couple of decades in earnest, researchers have pondered the construction of a “space elevator” between Earth and distant space. Now, several scientists–and executives at one major Japanese company–believe the idea has wings.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 4:\nA 2019 report <https://www.heinleinbooks.com/product-page/road-to-the-space-elevator-era-1> by the International Academy of Astronautics  <https://iaaspace.org/about/iaa-in-brief/#About-AtAGlance>said “a broad group of space professionals” concluded that the space elevator seemed not only feasible but that the “development initiation is nearer than most think.” Still, Cohen says, “I think an appetite to build a space elevator is sort of the wildcard in this,” and there certainly has been no heavy public or governmental push for the project.\n\nTo Edwards, that’s where more widespread information would help, including more details on how close the idea is to reality. “We can build it now,” he says. “It is an economic win now.”\n\nIt isn’t, of course, until entities invest. But for the first time in nearly 150 years, the space elevator is out of the realm of sci-fi and into the orbit of mission possible. Stuck in space, waiting for a rocket launch to bring them home, Suni Williams and Barry Wilmore might have been thrilled to have the option of just pushing the down button.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 4:\nToo, such mass-transport deliveries could prime the pump for asteroid mining, building a village on the moon, and establishing space-based solar power that could beam clean power to, say, New York or France. All might begin to be realized via a system that can get massive amounts of material to and from space quickly and at a low enough cost not to scare away investors, companies, or governments.\n\nTheoretically.\n\nIf this sounds like something a novelist might imagine, understand that it once was. The late sci-fi writer Arthur C. Clarke made the construction of a space elevator the centerpiece of his novel The Fountains of Paradise in 1979, almost half a century ago. In 2001, Clark wrote to Edwards to say he’d once predicted that it would be “50 years after everyone stops laughing” before the elevator would be built. After reading Edwards’ NASA report, Clark wrote, “They just stopped.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 4:\nEdwards hopes so. His new company, Industrial CNT <https://carbonnanotubes.org>, is in the process of rounding up funding to make longer and longer carbon nanotubes, which eventually could form the tether. He believes a space elevator could be completed in eight to 10 years, including the time it would take to ramp up the carbon nanotube production. (Other experts suggest the use of graphene and note that China has been making large graphene molecules.)\n\nWhoever develops this first is going to control space\n\n“I think whoever develops this infrastructure first is going to really control space,” says ISEC’s Wright, whose next conference <https://www.isec.org/events/isec2024> is set for early September in Chicago. “If that message can be brought out in America, then I think people would be more willing to look at the idea and say, well, I think some people are actually working on it. We should be too, otherwise, we\'re going to be behind the eight ball.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 5:\nThe transportation revolution isn\'t confined to the ground. Pioneers including Joby Aviation and EHang are taking to the skies, with air taxis set to become as ubiquitous as yellow cabs. EHang\'s test flights in Dubai offer a sneak peek into a future in which urban mobility reaches new heights, reshaping cityscapes across the globe. In the coming years, robotic housekeepers will be as common as dishwashers—vacuuming, cooking, cleaning, and providing medical care.\nHowever, as I posited in my book The Driver in the Driverless Car, the costs of the bright, shiny convenience that dangles from a convergence of AI, robotics, and the Internet of Things may be higher than we can readily foresee. The widespread use of each of these classes of technology raises critical questions about privacy, security, and employment. In entering an era in which every appliance is smart, every room is connected, and every task is automated, the tantalizing convenience machines entering our lives may come with unimaginable risks leading to irreversible losses of those things we consider most fundamental: freedom, spontaneity, communion with nature, as well as our capacities to heal naturally, think expansively, express new thoughts and feelings without inhibition, and act from true generosity.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/10/future-humanity-risk-science-fiction-ai-tech/\n\nSource 6:\nStill, Williams said, “This is my happy place…It’s very peaceful up here a lot of times. One persuasive answer to this viewpoint is offered by Herbert F. York, the young physicist who is Director of Defense Research and Engineering. Space, however, is much less hedged about with secrecy than the atom was in 1953 and 1954, and it offers a far wider range of technical challenges. Moreover, the investment needed to make a useful contribution to space technology, especially its electronic aspects, is far smaller than that needed to contribute to nuclear technology. For example, the instruments that James Van Allen used to detect the great belts of radiation that now bear his name were built in a basement of the physics department at the State University of Iowa. It’s a fast-growing market as launch costs go down and demand for data, sensing, and software rises. Therefore, as economic ties increase, and space access becomes more affordable, the space economy will impact the value and resilience of almost all industries across the world.\n\nThe U.S. will maintain its lead—but China\'s bold space program aims to challenge American power militarily, economically, and diplomatically. To counter China and its allies’ growing presence in space, there needs to be a strong transatlantic effort. "It\'s going to open up access to places like the Moon at scale to enable industrial activity," he said. \n\nWhile Astrolab may not be a direct competitor to Blue Origin and SpaceX, another startup—Stoke Space—is working on similar technology with the aim of building fully reusable rockets. \n\nSpeaking at Brainstorm Tech, Stoke\'s chief operating officer Kelly Hennig admitted that she might not beat SpaceX to the goal, but that space onlookers should "vote for the little man, too." She said that as the space landscape evolves, there will be a need for both large freight companies as well as smaller firms, comparing Stoke to the Sprinter vans used by Amazon <https://fortune.com/company/amazon-com/>. A SpaceX <https://fortune.com/company/spacex/> rescue craft, meanwhile, couldn’t reach the astronauts until February 2025.\n\nThe only option for Williams and Wilmore was to wait. But that may not always be the case. And if and when the scenario changes, it may well be in a direction that is quite literally the stuff of science fiction.\n\nThe technology is there\n\nFor more than a century in theory and at least a couple of decades in earnest, researchers have pondered the construction of a “space elevator” between Earth and distant space. Now, several scientists–and executives at one major Japanese company–believe the idea has wings.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 4:\nA 2019 report <https://www.heinleinbooks.com/product-page/road-to-the-space-elevator-era-1> by the International Academy of Astronautics  <https://iaaspace.org/about/iaa-in-brief/#About-AtAGlance>said “a broad group of space professionals” concluded that the space elevator seemed not only feasible but that the “development initiation is nearer than most think.” Still, Cohen says, “I think an appetite to build a space elevator is sort of the wildcard in this,” and there certainly has been no heavy public or governmental push for the project.\n\nTo Edwards, that’s where more widespread information would help, including more details on how close the idea is to reality. “We can build it now,” he says. “It is an economic win now.”\n\nIt isn’t, of course, until entities invest. But for the first time in nearly 150 years, the space elevator is out of the realm of sci-fi and into the orbit of mission possible. Stuck in space, waiting for a rocket launch to bring them home, Suni Williams and Barry Wilmore might have been thrilled to have the option of just pushing the down button.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 4:\nToo, such mass-transport deliveries could prime the pump for asteroid mining, building a village on the moon, and establishing space-based solar power that could beam clean power to, say, New York or France. All might begin to be realized via a system that can get massive amounts of material to and from space quickly and at a low enough cost not to scare away investors, companies, or governments.\n\nTheoretically.\n\nIf this sounds like something a novelist might imagine, understand that it once was. The late sci-fi writer Arthur C. Clarke made the construction of a space elevator the centerpiece of his novel The Fountains of Paradise in 1979, almost half a century ago. In 2001, Clark wrote to Edwards to say he’d once predicted that it would be “50 years after everyone stops laughing” before the elevator would be built. After reading Edwards’ NASA report, Clark wrote, “They just stopped.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 4:\nEdwards hopes so. His new company, Industrial CNT <https://carbonnanotubes.org>, is in the process of rounding up funding to make longer and longer carbon nanotubes, which eventually could form the tether. He believes a space elevator could be completed in eight to 10 years, including the time it would take to ramp up the carbon nanotube production. (Other experts suggest the use of graphene and note that China has been making large graphene molecules.)\n\nWhoever develops this first is going to control space\n\n“I think whoever develops this infrastructure first is going to really control space,” says ISEC’s Wright, whose next conference <https://www.isec.org/events/isec2024> is set for early September in Chicago. “If that message can be brought out in America, then I think people would be more willing to look at the idea and say, well, I think some people are actually working on it. We should be too, otherwise, we\'re going to be behind the eight ball.”\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/04/astronauts-stuck-space-solution-elevator-science-tech/\n\nSource 5:\nThe transportation revolution isn\'t confined to the ground. Pioneers including Joby Aviation and EHang are taking to the skies, with air taxis set to become as ubiquitous as yellow cabs. EHang\'s test flights in Dubai offer a sneak peek into a future in which urban mobility reaches new heights, reshaping cityscapes across the globe. In the coming years, robotic housekeepers will be as common as dishwashers—vacuuming, cooking, cleaning, and providing medical care.\nHowever, as I posited in my book The Driver in the Driverless Car, the costs of the bright, shiny convenience that dangles from a convergence of AI, robotics, and the Internet of Things may be higher than we can readily foresee. The widespread use of each of these classes of technology raises critical questions about privacy, security, and employment. In entering an era in which every appliance is smart, every room is connected, and every task is automated, the tantalizing convenience machines entering our lives may come with unimaginable risks leading to irreversible losses of those things we consider most fundamental: freedom, spontaneity, communion with nature, as well as our capacities to heal naturally, think expansively, express new thoughts and feelings without inhibition, and act from true generosity.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/10/future-humanity-risk-science-fiction-ai-tech/\n\nSource 6:\nStill, Williams said, “This is my happy place…It’s very peaceful up here a lot of times. In the coming years, robotic housekeepers will be as common as dishwashers—vacuuming, cooking, cleaning, and providing medical care.\nHowever, as I posited in my book The Driver in the Driverless Car, the costs of the bright, shiny convenience that dangles from a convergence of AI, robotics, and the Internet of Things may be higher than we can readily foresee. The widespread use of each of these classes of technology raises critical questions about privacy, security, and employment. In entering an era in which every appliance is smart, every room is connected, and every task is automated, the tantalizing convenience machines entering our lives may come with unimaginable risks leading to irreversible losses of those things we consider most fundamental: freedom, spontaneity, communion with nature, as well as our capacities to heal naturally, think expansively, express new thoughts and feelings without inhibition, and act from true generosity.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/10/future-humanity-risk-science-fiction-ai-tech/\n\nSource 6:\nStill, Williams said, “This is my happy place…It’s very peaceful up here a lot of times. There’s a lot of work that’s going on, but it also gives you a little time to be introspective.” Back on Earth, it falls to NASA’s decision-makers to figure out the next steps—and how to go to space without its astronauts being stranded there.\n\nMore must-read commentary published by Fortune:\nMore must-read commentary published by Fortune:\n\n * Inflation, housing, immigration, taxes: The Harris-Walz economic policy scorecard <https://fortune.com/2024/08/29/inflation-housing-immigration-taxes-the-harris-walz-economic-policy-scorecard/> \n * The ‘sustainability recession’ <https://fortune.com/2024/08/14/sustainability-recession-end-soon-esg-dei-politics-corporate-leadership/> will end soon—and not by choice \n * ‘Godmother of AI’ says California’s well-intended AI bill will harm the U.S. ecosystem <https://fortune.com/2024/08/06/godmother-of-ai-says-californias-ai-bill-will-harm-us-ecosystem-tech-politics/> \n * The most underrated leadership skill, according to Jake Sullivan <https://fortune.com/2024/08/16/most-underrated-skill-leadership-jake-sullivan/?utm_source=search&utm_medium=suggested_search&utm_campaign=search_link_clicks> \nThe opinions expressed in Fortune.com commentary pieces are solely the views of their authors and do not necessarily reflect the opinions and beliefs of Fortune.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/09/18/the-stranded-astronauts-are-stoic-nasa-shouldnt-be/'}

2024-11-08 06:49:19,559: ObserveEventsHandler(  198): MEASUREMENT: LLMChatInProgress response: assistant: If space travel became commonplace, it would likely have a significant impact on various aspects of our lives. For one, it would open up new opportunities for economic growth, as the space industry is expected to reach $1.8 trillion by 2035 [2]. This growth would not only benefit the companies directly involved in space travel but also have a ripple effect on other industries, such as logistics and manufacturing. For instance, companies like Astrolab are already working on developing rovers that can operate on interplanetary bodies, which could enable industrial activity on the moon and other celestial bodies [3].

Furthermore, space travel becoming commonplace would also raise questions about the governance and regulation of space. As more countries and private companies gain access to space, there would be a need for international cooperation and agreements to ensure that space is used responsibly and safely [2]. This could lead to the development of new laws and regulations, as well as the establishment of international organizations to oversee space activities. Additionally, the increased accessibility of space could also lead to new opportunities for scientific research and exploration, as scientists and researchers would have more opportunities to conduct experiments and gather data in space [1].

The impact of space travel becoming commonplace would also be felt on a societal level. As space travel becomes more accessible, it could lead to new forms of tourism and entertainment, such as space vacations and asteroid mining [4]. This could also lead to new opportunities for education and outreach, as people would have more opportunities to learn about space and experience it firsthand. However, it's also important to consider the potential risks and challenges associated with space travel, such as the effects of long-term spaceflight on the human body and the potential for accidents or malfunctions [6].

This could also lead to new opportunities for education and outreach, as people would have more opportunities to learn about space and experience it firsthand. However, it's also important to consider the potential risks and challenges associated with space travel, such as the effects of long-term spaceflight on the human body and the potential for accidents or malfunctions [6]. As space travel becomes more common, it would be essential to address these challenges and ensure that space travel is safe and sustainable for everyone involved.
